### Problem Understanding

The problem requires reconstructing an array `a` of exactly 3 positive integers from a given sorted array `b` of 7 integers, where `b` contains the sorted sums of all non-empty subsequences of `a`. There are `2^3 - 1 = 7` such subsequences, and their sums form `b` after sorting in non-decreasing order. The input guarantees at least one valid `a` exists, and any valid `a` can be output. The array `a` elements are positive integers (≥1), `b` is non-decreasing, and values can be up to 10^9, with `t ≤ 5000` test cases.

Key insights:
- Let `s = b[6]` (0-indexed, the largest sum, which is always `a[0] + a[1] + a[2]`).
- The sums consist of: three single elements `{x, y, z}`, three pair sums `{x+y, x+z, y+z}` which equal `{s - z, s - y, s - x}`, and `s`.
- Thus, the first 6 elements of `b` form the multiset `{x, y, z, s-x, s-y, s-z}`.
- The singles `x, y, z` must sum to `s` (since `x + y + z = s`), and the remaining three in `b[0:6]` must exactly match `{s-x, s-y, s-z}` as a multiset.
- Duplicates are possible (e.g., a single can equal a pair sum), so we must handle multisets carefully.
- Since `|b[0:6]| = 6` and we choose 3 for singles, brute-force checking all `C(6,3) = 20` combinations is efficient.

This structure allows systematic recovery without assuming order in `a`.

### Recall Example Problem

**Problem Description:**  
LeetCode 1: Two Sum. You are given an array of integers `nums` (length `n ≤ 10^4`, values `-10^9 ≤ nums[i] ≤ 10^9`) and an integer `target` (`-10^18 ≤ target ≤ 10^18`). Return the indices (1-based in some variants, 0-based here) of two distinct elements `nums[i]` and `nums[j]` (i ≠ j) such that `nums[i] + nums[j] = target`. Assume exactly one solution exists, and return in any order. This is a classic problem for hashmap-based lookups to achieve O(n) time.

**Generate Python3 code step by step to solve that problem:**

1. Import necessary modules: No imports needed beyond built-ins.

2. Define the function signature: `def twoSum(nums: List[int], target: int) -> List[int]:`

3. Initialize a dictionary `seen = {}` to store `num: index` mappings for O(1) lookups.

4. Iterate over the array with `enumerate` for indices:
   ```
   for i, num in enumerate(nums):
   ```

5. For each `num`, compute the complement `complement = target - num`.

6. Check if `complement` exists in `seen`:
   ```
   if complement in seen:
       return [seen[complement], i]
   ```

7. After the check, add current `num: i` to `seen` (to avoid using the same index).

8. Close the function. Since guaranteed solution, no need for return `[]`.

Full code:
```python
from typing import List

def twoSum(nums: List[int], target: int) -> List[int]:
    seen = {}
    for i, num in enumerate(nums):
        complement = target - num
        if complement in seen:
            return [seen[complement], i]
        seen[num] = i
    return []  # Though assumed not reached
```

**Discuss the algorithm to solve this problem:**  
The algorithm uses a single-pass hashmap (dictionary) to track seen numbers and their indices. For each element, it checks if the required complement (`target - current`) has been seen before. If yes, return the pair of indices. This avoids O(n^2) brute force, achieving O(n) time and O(n) space. Key: add to hashmap *after* checking to prevent self-pairing. Handles duplicates fine since indices differ. Edge cases: negatives, zeros, large values (Python dict handles big ints).

**Finally generate a planning to solve that problem:**  
1. Read input: `n` (optional), `nums` list, `target`.  
2. Initialize empty dict `seen`.  
3. Loop over indices `i` from 0 to `n-1`:  
   - Compute `complement = target - nums[i]`.  
   - If `complement` in `seen`, output `[seen[complement], i]`.  
   - Else, set `seen[nums[i]] = i`.  
4. Output the indices (sorted if required, but any order ok).  
5. Verify: time O(n), space O(n), correct by construction.

### Algorithm to solve the original problem

**Algorithm:** Brute-force combination search with multiset validation.  
- For each test case:  
  1. Read `b = [b1, ..., b7]`, set `s = b[6]` (0-indexed).  
  2. Consider `prefix = b[0:6]`.  
  3. Generate all combinations of 3 distinct indices from 0 to 5 (C(6,3)=20).  
  4. For each combo `(i,j,k)`:  
     - Let `singles = sorted([prefix[i], prefix[j], prefix[k]])`.  
     - If `sum(singles) != s`, skip.  
     - Compute `pairs = sorted([s - singles[0], s - singles[1], s - singles[2]])`.  
     - Collect remaining elements in `prefix` excluding indices i,j,k, sort them as `rem`.  
     - If `pairs == rem`, then `a = singles` is valid; output it (any order).  
- Stop at first valid (guaranteed ≥1).  

**Tutorials about the algorithm:**  
- **How to approach this type of algorithm:** This is "reconstruction via multiset matching" or "inverse subset sum" for small fixed size (n=3). For small k (here k=3 choices from m=6), exhaustive search over combinations is optimal (O(1) per test). Use itertools.combinations for indices. Validate by recomputing derived values (pairs) and checking equality after sorting (handles duplicates). Generalize to larger n via meet-in-middle or DP, but unnecessary here.  
- **Important things to consider:**  
  - Multisets: Always sort lists before comparing to ignore order/duplicates.  
  - Indices vs values: Iterate combinations of *indices* to handle duplicate values in `b`.  
  - Positivity: Guaranteed by input (no need to check a_i ≥1).  
  - Large values: Use 64-bit ints (Python handles seamlessly, no overflow).  
  - Efficiency: 5000 * 20 * O(1) = negligible.  
  - Multiple solutions: Output any; first found is fine.  
  - Verification: Optionally check full sorted sums match `b`, but unnecessary due to guarantee.

### Plan

- **Step 1: Read input.**  
  Read `t` (number of test cases). For each of `t` cases: read line with 7 integers into list `b` (0-indexed, `b[0] ≤ ... ≤ b[6]`).

- **Step 2: For each test case, set `s = b[6]` (total sum). Prepare `prefix = b[:6]`.**

- **Step 3: Generate all possible combinations of 3 indices from range(6).**  
  Use nested loops or library equivalent: for i in 0..3, for j in i+1..4, for k in j+1..5 (20 combos).

- **Step 4: For each combination (i,j,k):**  
  - Extract candidate singles: `cand = [prefix[i], prefix[j], prefix[k]]`.  
  - If `cand[0] + cand[1] + cand[2] != s`, continue to next combo.  
  - Sort `cand` to `singles = sorted(cand)`.  
  - Compute expected pairs: `expected_pairs = sorted([s - singles[0], s - singles[1], s - singles[2]])`.  
  - Collect remaining indices: the three not in {i,j,k}, say set(range(6)) - {i,j,k}, extract `rem = [prefix[m] for m in remaining]`, sort to `sorted_rem`.  
  - If `expected_pairs == sorted_rem`, this is valid: set `a = singles` (or unsorted cand), break.

- **Step 5: Output the `a` elements.**  
  Print `a[0] a[1] a[2]` separated by spaces (any order ok, e.g., original cand order or sorted).

- **Step 6: Repeat for all t cases.**  
  Ensure no extra spaces/newlines.

- **Edge cases to handle implicitly:**  
  - All equal (e.g., 300M case): combos will match.  
  - Duplicates (e.g., singles=1,1,large): sorting handles.  
  - Single equals pair (e.g., first example 4=4): multiset ok.  
  - Minimal values (1s): fine.  
  - Maximal (1e9): Python int ok.

This plan is complete, efficient, and directly implements the algorithm.

```python
import sys
from itertools import combinations

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

idx = 0
t = int(data[idx])
idx += 1

for _ in range(t):
    b = [int(data[idx + i]) for i in range(7)]
    idx += 7
    s = b[6]
    for comb in combinations(range(6), 3):
        cand = [b[i] for i in comb]
        if sum(cand) == s:
            exp_pairs = sorted(s - x for x in cand)
            rem = [b[i] for i in range(6) if i not in comb]
            if sorted(rem) == exp_pairs:
                print(cand[0], cand[1], cand[2])
                break
```

### Problem Understanding

The problem requires determining if a given string `s` is "square," meaning it consists of some string `x` repeated exactly twice (i.e., `s = x + x`). This implies:
- The length of `s` (denoted `n`) must be even, as odd lengths cannot be split into two equal parts.
- If `n` is even, let `mid = n // 2`; then `s` is square if and only if `s[:mid] == s[mid:]`.
- Strings consist of lowercase letters only, with `1 ≤ n ≤ 100` and `1 ≤ t ≤ 100`, so efficiency is not a concern (brute-force or simple checks suffice).
- Edge cases: `n=1` (odd, NO), `n=2` (YES if both chars equal), empty string (not possible per constraints), repeated patterns like "aaaa" (YES, as "aa"+"aa").
- Output "YES" or "NO" (case-insensitive acceptance).

Examples confirm: "aa" (YES), "aaa" (NO, odd), "abab" (YES), "xxyy" (NO, halves differ).

### Recall Example Problem

**Description**: A different but relevant problem is determining if a string is a **palindrome**. A palindrome is a string that reads the same forwards and backwards (e.g., "aba", "racecar", "aa"). Given multiple test cases, check for each string if it is a palindrome. Input: first line integer `t` (1 ≤ t ≤ 100), then `t` lines each with a string of lowercase letters (length 1-100). Output "YES" or "NO" for each (case-insensitive).

**Generate Python3 code step by step to solve that problem**:

1. Import necessary modules (none needed here).
2. Read the number of test cases `t` using `int(input())`.
3. Loop `t` times:
   a. Read the string `s` using `input().strip()` to handle any whitespace.
   b. Check if `s` is a palindrome by comparing `s` with its reverse `s[::-1]`.
   c. If equal, print "YES"; else, print "NO".
4. Full code structure ensures it handles all cases efficiently.

```python
# Step-by-step assembled code
t = int(input())
for _ in range(t):
    s = input().strip()
    if s == s[::-1]:
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
```

**Discuss the algorithm to solve this problem**:
- **Core idea**: Compare the string with its reverse. Python's slicing `s[::-1]` creates the reverse in O(n) time.
- **Time/Space**: O(n) time per test case (slicing + comparison), O(n) space for reverse (but optimizable with two pointers).
- **Alternatives**: Two-pointer approach (left from 0, right from n-1, move towards center, compare chars) avoids extra space, still O(n).
- **Strengths**: Simple, handles all cases (odd/even length, single char YES, empty YES but not in constraints).
- **Edge cases**: Single char (YES), all same chars (YES), odd length (check center implicitly).

**Finally generate a planning to solve that problem**:
1. Parse input: Read `t`.
2. For each of `t` test cases:
   a. Read string `s`.
   b. Compute length `n = len(s)`.
   c. Use two pointers: `left = 0`, `right = n-1`.
   d. While `left < right`: if `s[left] != s[right]`, it's NO; else increment `left`, decrement `right`.
   e. If loop completes without mismatch, YES.
3. Output "YES" or "NO" per test case.
4. Test with examples: "aba" (YES), "abc" (NO), "a" (YES).

### Algorithm to solve the original problem

**Algorithm**:
1. For each test case:
   a. Read string `s`, compute `n = len(s)`.
   b. If `n % 2 != 0`, return "NO" (cannot split evenly).
   c. Else, `mid = n // 2`.
   d. Check if `s[:mid] == s[mid:]`.
   e. If yes, "YES"; else "NO".

**Tutorials about the algorithm**:
- **How to approach this type of algorithm** (string half-comparison/repetition check):
  - Recognize pattern: "Square" is a specific case of period-2 repetition. Generalize to check if string has period `k` where `2k = n`.
  - Use string slicing: In Python, `s[:mid]` and `s[mid:]` are O(mid) but fine for n≤100.
  - Validate preconditions first (even length) to early-exit.
  - For similar problems (k-repetitions): Check `s[i] == s[i + step]` for all i, but here step = n/2 simplifies to half-equality.
- **Important things to consider**:
  - Length parity: Always check `n % 2 == 0` first.
  - Zero-length halves: n=0 not possible, but n=2 works.
  - No leading/trailing spaces: Use `strip()` if needed, but inputs are clean.
  - Case/output: Print uppercase "YES"/"NO"; judge accepts variants.
  - Efficiency: O(n) per test case, total O(100*100)=10^4, trivial.
  - Common pitfalls: Odd lengths ("aaa"), unequal halves ("xxyy"), non-prefix repeats ("abcdabc" fails as halves "abcd"!="abc").

### Plan

1. **Input parsing**:
   a. Read integer `t` using `int(input())`.
   b. Initialize a loop for `range(t)` to process each test case.

2. **Per test case processing**:
   a. Read the string `s` using `input().strip()` to remove any potential whitespace.
   b. Compute `n = len(s)`.
   c. Check if `n % 2 != 0`: if true, output "NO" and proceed to next test case.
   d. If even, compute `mid = n // 2`.
   e. Extract first half: `first = s[:mid]`.
   f. Extract second half: `second = s[mid:]`.
   g. Compare `first == second`: if true, output "YES"; else "NO".

3. **Output handling**:
   a. Ensure each output on a new line.
   b. Use consistent casing ("YES", "NO") for reliability.

4. **Verification steps** (during implementation):
   a. Test odd length: e.g., "a" → NO, "aaa" → NO.
   b. Test even equal halves: "aa" → YES, "aaaa" → YES, "abcabc" → YES.
   c. Test even unequal: "xxyy" → NO, "xyyx" → NO.
   d. Test pattern repeats: "abab" → YES ("ab"+"ab"), "xyxy" → YES.
   e. Run full example input to match outputs.

5. **Optimization/robustness**:
   a. No need for loops/slicing alternatives since n small.
   b. Avoid mutable strings or regex (overkill).
   c. Handle t=1 and t=100 edge counts.

6. **Final assembly**:
   a. Wrap in main loop.
   b. No global variables needed.

### Problem Understanding
The problem requires determining whether there exists an array of positive integers `a[1..n]` arranged in a circle that satisfies equality (`E`) or inequality (`N`) constraints between every pair of neighboring elements, including the pair `(a_n, a_1)`. The constraints are given as a string `s` of length `n` (2 ≤ n ≤ 50), where `s[i-1]` specifies the relation between `a_i` and `a_{i+1}` (with wrap-around). Multiple test cases (t ≤ 1000) must be handled efficiently, but small constraints allow O(n) or O(n α(n)) solutions.

Key insights:
- `E` forces neighboring elements to be equal, forming groups (connected components) that must share the same value.
- `N` forces neighboring elements to be unequal.
- Inconsistencies arise if an `N` constraint exists between positions forced to be equal (same group).
- Since values are unbounded positive integers (up to 10^9 suffices if possible), inequalities between different groups can always be satisfied by assigning distinct values.
- The circular nature requires handling wrap-around for both unions and checks.
- No need to construct the array; just check consistency.

Examples confirm:
- All `E`: One group, possible.
- `EN` (n=2): Unions make all equal, but `N` contradicts.
- Chains of `E` merging across circle can cause contradictions with `N`.

This reduces to grouping via equalities and verifying inequalities don't cross groups.

### Recall Example Problem
**Recalled Problem: LeetCode 990 - Satisfiability of Equality Equations**  
This problem involves an array of strings `equations` (e.g., `["a==b","b!=a"]`), where each string is either `"xi==yi"` or `"xi!=yi"`, and variables are lowercase letters (a-z). Determine if there exists a mapping from variables to integers (0-1000) that satisfies all equations. Output `true` if possible, `false` otherwise. Constraints: up to 500 equations, 26 variables.

This is relevant as it uses the same core idea: union variables connected by `==` into components (must have same value), then check if any `!=` connects variables in the same component (contradiction). Differs from the original as it handles arbitrary variables/equations (not a circle) and limited value range (but unlimited effectively).

**Generate Python3 code step by step to solve that problem:**

1. **Implement Union-Find (Disjoint Set Union - DSU) structure**: Need path compression and union by rank for efficiency.
   ```python
   class UnionFind:
       def __init__(self, size):
           self.parent = list(range(size))
           self.rank = [0] * size
   
       def find(self, x):
           if self.parent[x] != x:
               self.parent[x] = self.find(self.parent[x])  # Path compression
           return self.parent[x]
   
       def union(self, x, y):
           px, py = self.find(x), self.find(y)
           if px != py:
               if self.rank[px] < self.rank[py]:
                   self.parent[px] = py
               elif self.rank[px] > self.rank[py]:
                   self.parent[py] = px
               else:
                   self.parent[py] = px
                   self.rank[px] += 1
   ```

2. **Map variables to indices**: Since a-z, use ord(c) - ord('a') for 0-25.
   ```python
   def equationsPossible(equations):
       uf = UnionFind(26)
   ```

3. **First pass: Process all == equations**: Union the variables.
   ```python
       equals = [eq for eq in equations if eq[1] == '=']  # Filter ==
       for eq in equals:
           x, y = ord(eq[0]) - ord('a'), ord(eq[3]) - ord('a')
           uf.union(x, y)
   ```

4. **Second pass: Check all != equations**: Verify not same parent.
   ```python
       for eq in equations:
           if eq[1] == '!':
               x, y = ord(eq[0]) - ord('a'), ord(eq[3]) - ord('a')
               if uf.find(x) == uf.find(y):
                   return False
       return True
   ```

5. **Full function wrapper**:
   ```python
   # Complete code as above, wrapped in the function.
   ```

**Discuss the algorithm to solve this problem:**  
The algorithm uses Union-Find to group variables that must be equal (`==` edges form equivalence classes). After processing all unions, iterate through `!=` constraints: if endpoints share a root (forced equal), it's impossible. Time: O(m α(26)) ≈ O(m), where m=equations (path compression + union-by-rank nearly constant). Space: O(26). Correctness: Captures transitive equality (e.g., a==b, b==c → a==c), detects contradictions directly. Handles disconnected components fine (assign distinct values if needed).

**Finally generate a planning to solve that problem:**  
1. Create UnionFind class with find (path compression) and union (by rank).  
2. Initialize UF for 26 variables.  
3. Collect and process all `==` equations: map chars to indices, union pairs.  
4. Iterate all `!=` equations: if find(x) == find(y), return false.  
5. If no contradictions, return true.  
6. Edge cases: No equations (true), self-equality (true), cycles of equality with != (false).

### Algorithm to solve the original problem
**Algorithm: Union-Find with Equality Grouping and Inequality Checks**  
- Model positions 0 to n-1 as nodes.  
- Use Union-Find: For each `s[i] == 'E'`, union i and (i+1)%n (transitively merge equal groups).  
- After all unions, for each `s[i] == 'N'`, if find(i) == find((i+1)%n), impossible (forced equal but != required).  
- If no such violation, possible (assign same value per component, distinct for adjacent differing components using distinct integers).  
Time: O(n α(n)) per test case (α(50) ≈ 1), total O(t n). Space: O(n).  

**Tutorials about the algorithm:**  
- **How to approach this type of algorithm**: Identify equality constraints first (build components via unions), defer inequalities (check post-union). Use DSU for efficient equivalence class management in graphs/constraints. Process circle via %n. Separate passes ensure all transitive equalities propagate before checks.  
- **Important things to consider**:  
  - Path compression/union-by-rank for efficiency (though n=50 trivial).  
  - Handle circle: (i+1) % n for both union and check.  
  - Only contradictions are `N` within same component; no global coloring needed (unlimited values).  
  - Edge cases: n=2 (`EE` YES, `EN`/`NE`/`NN` handle correctly); all `E` (YES); all `N` (YES); odd cycle of `N` (YES, unlike 2-coloring).  
  - Resources: CP-Algorithms DSU tutorial; LeetCode 990/200/547 for similar uses.

### Plan
1. **Read input**: Parse t, then for each test case, read string s (n = len(s)).  
2. **Initialize Union-Find**: Create parent[0..n-1] = range(n), rank[0..n-1] = 0. Implement find (with path compression) and union (by rank).  
3. **Process equalities (first pass)**: For i in 0 to n-1: if s[i] == 'E', union(i, (i+1) % n).  
4. **Check inequalities (second pass)**: For i in 0 to n-1: if s[i] == 'N' and find(i) == find((i+1) % n), mark as NO (break early if desired).  
5. **Output**: If no violations, YES; else NO. Repeat for all t cases.  
6. **Handle multiple test cases**: Use fast input if needed (but Python fine for t=1000, n=50).  
7. **Verification**: Test on samples (e.g., "EEE" → all unioned, no N → YES; "EN" → union 0-1, N on 1-0 same → NO; "ENNEENE" → compute components, verify no N intra-component → YES).  
8. **Implement robustly**: 0-based indexing, %n for circle, uppercase YES/NO. No array construction needed.

### Plan Evaluation
**No Need to Modify Plan**.
```python
import sys

def find(parent, x):
    if parent[x] != x:
        parent[x] = find(parent, parent[x])
    return parent[x]

def union(parent, rank, x, y):
    px = find(parent, x)
    py = find(parent, y)
    if px != py:
        if rank[px] < rank[py]:
            parent[px] = py
        elif rank[px] > rank[py]:
            parent[py] = px
        else:
            parent[py] = px
            rank[px] += 1

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
idx = 1
for _ in range(t):
    s = data[idx]
    idx += 1
    n = len(s)
    parent = list(range(n))
    rank = [0] * n
    
    # Process equalities
    for i in range(n):
        if s[i] == 'E':
            union(parent, rank, i, (i + 1) % n)
    
    # Check inequalities
    possible = True
    for i in range(n):
        if s[i] == 'N' and find(parent, i) == find(parent, (i + 1) % n):
            possible = False
            break
    
    print("YES" if possible else "NO")
```

### Problem Understanding

The problem requires determining whether it is possible to break exactly one of three given sticks with integer lengths \(l_1, l_2, l_3\) (each between 1 and \(10^8\)) into two pieces of positive integer lengths that sum to the original length, resulting in four sticks that can form a rectangle (including a square). A rectangle requires exactly two pairs of equal side lengths (i.e., the multiset of four lengths must be of the form \(\{a, a, b, b\}\) for positive integers \(a, b\)).

Key constraints:
- Exactly one stick is broken into two positive integers \(x, y\) with \(x \geq 1\), \(y \geq 1\), \(x + y =\) original length (so original must be at least 2).
- There are \(t \leq 10^4\) test cases, and lengths up to \(10^8\), so the solution must be \(O(1)\) or \(O(t)\) time, avoiding loops over possible break points.
- Output "YES" or "NO" (case-insensitive) for each test case.

Initial insights:
- The four lengths after breaking must have exactly two distinct values, each appearing twice (or one value four times).
- Brute-forcing break points is impossible due to large lengths.
- Case analysis is needed: choose which stick to break, then check if the resulting lengths can pair into two equal pairs.
- Possible pairings are limited: either the two pieces are equal (and match the other two equal sticks), or the pieces match the other two sticks individually.

### Recall Example Problem

**Recalled Problem: "Triangle Formation"**  
This is a classic problem distinct from the given one: Given \(t\) test cases (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^5), each with three positive integers \(a, b, c\) (1 ≤ a, b, c ≤ 10^9) representing stick lengths, determine if it is possible to form a non-degenerate triangle using exactly these three sticks. Output "YES" if the lengths satisfy the triangle inequality (strict: sum of any two sides > third side), otherwise "NO". (This differs as it involves three sticks forming a triangle without breaking any, focusing on inequalities rather than pairing for a rectangle or breaking sticks.)

   ```python
       index += 3
   ```

   ```python
       sides = sorted([a, b, c])

   ```

   ```python
           print("YES")
   ```

**Full assembled code (for reference, though not executed here):**  
The steps combine into a complete efficient script handling large inputs without TLE.

**Discuss the algorithm to solve this problem:**  
The algorithm uses case analysis via sorting: sort the sides \(a \leq b \leq c\), then verify the strict triangle inequality \(a + b > c\) (the other two \(a + c > b\), \(b + c > a\) hold automatically due to sorting and positivity). Time complexity: \(O(t \log 3) = O(t)\) since sorting three elements is constant. Space: \(O(1)\) per test. It's optimal, handles large numbers (Python ints arbitrary precision), and avoids floating points.

**Finally generate a planning to solve that problem:**  
1. Use fast input to read all data at once (sys.stdin.read) for speed with t=1e5.  
2. Parse t, then for each test case: extract next three integers as a,b,c.

4. Check if a + b > c; if yes, output "YES", else "NO".  
5. Ensure output is case-consistent (e.g., uppercase).  
6. Test edge cases: all equal (YES, equilateral), a+b=c (NO, degenerate), min lengths (1,1,1: YES), max (1e9 cases: no overflow).

### Algorithm to solve the original problem

**Algorithm:**  
For each test case with lengths \(l_1, l_2, l_3\):  
- Consider each of the three sticks as the one to break (r), with the other two as p and q.  
- For a fixed choice (p, q, r): it is possible if \(r \geq 2\) and either:  
  1. p == q and r is even (break r into r/2, r/2; forms {p, p, r/2, r/2}).  
  2. p + q == r (break r into p, q; forms {p, q, p, q}).  
- If the condition holds for **any** of the three choices, output "YES"; else "NO".  

This covers all pairing cases via exhaustive choice of break stick (only 3) and pairing logic (equal pieces or matching others). Time: O(1) per test case, total O(t). Correctness from case analysis: no other ways to form two pairs exist.

**Give some tutorials about the algorithm:**  
- **How to approach this type of algorithm:** This is a **case analysis + brute-force over small choices** algorithm, ideal for constraint satisfaction with few decisions (here, 3 choices for which to break) and large ranges (can't loop over splits). Identify all minimal configurations (equal split or sum-match), verify mathematically without enumeration. Sort inputs if helpful (not needed here), test exhaustively on small choices, prove no misses via pigeonhole on pairings.  
- **Important things to consider:**  
  - Edge cases: r=1 (can't break, conditions auto-fail); all 1s (NO); even/odd parity; p+q overflows? (Python handles 2*1e8 fine).  
  - Degeneracy: pieces must be positive integers (ensured by >=1 and sum=r).  
  - Rectangle validity: only pairing matters (no further inequalities needed, unlike triangles/quadrilaterals).  
  - Output case-insensitivity: standardize to "YES"/"NO".  
  - Efficiency: 3 checks per test, no loops/sorts needed.

### Plan

1. **Read input efficiently:** Use `import sys; input = sys.stdin.read; data = input().split()` to handle t=1e4 quickly. Parse t = int(data[0]), set index=1.  

2. **Loop over t test cases:** For _ in range(t): extract next three integers as l1 = int(data[index]), l2=int(data[index+1]), l3=int(data[index+2]); index +=3. Store in list L = [l1, l2, l3] for easy access.  

3. **Define a helper check function (conceptual, inline ok):** For given p, q, r: return (r >= 2) and ( (p == q and r % 2 == 0) or (p + q == r) ).  

4. **Check all three breaking options:**  
   - Option 1: break L[0], check(L[1], L[2], L[0])  
   - Option 2: break L[1], check(L[0], L[2], L[1])  
   - Option 3: break L[2], check(L[0], L[1], L[2])  
   If **any** returns True, set flag = True.  

5. **Output result:** If flag: print("YES") else print("NO").  

6. **Verification steps (during development):**  
   - Test examples: first (6,1,5: YES via 1+5=6), second (2,5,2: NO), etc.  
   - Edge: (1,1,1: NO), (1,1,2: YES via break 2 into 1,1), (2,2,2: YES via even), large (1e8,1e8,2e8: YES via sum).  
   - Ensure no modulo issues (r%2 for even r=1e8 ok).  

7. **Optimization/robustness:** No sorting needed (symmetric checks). Python3 handles all arithmetic natively. Total time <<1s.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    l1 = int(data[index])
    l2 = int(data[index + 1])
    l3 = int(data[index + 2])
    index += 3
    L = [l1, l2, l3]
    possible = False
    # break L[0]
    if L[0] >= 2 and ((L[1] == L[2] and L[0] % 2 == 0) or L[1] + L[2] == L[0]):

    # break L[1]

    # break L[2]
```

### Problem Understanding
The problem involves simulating a robot cleaner moving diagonally in a bounded rectangular grid (n rows by m columns) with wall reflections, starting from position (r_b, c_b) with initial direction (dr=1, dc=1). Each second (including t=0), the robot cleans its entire current row and column. The goal is to find the minimum time t >= 0 when the dirty cell (r_d, c_d) is cleaned, i.e., when the robot's row equals r_d or its column equals c_d. The robot's movement reflects independently on horizontal (row bounds 1/n) and vertical (column bounds 1/m) walls by reversing dr or dc before the step if the naive next position would be out of bounds. The grid is small (n, m <= 100), there are up to 10^4 test cases, and it's guaranteed the dirty cell is eventually cleaned. Key insight: row and column movements are completely independent due to separate reflection rules, allowing decomposition into two 1D bouncing problems. At t=0, check if already sharing row or column; otherwise, find the first t > 0 when either dimension hits its target and take the minimum.

### Recall Example Problem
**1D Bouncing Particle Problem** (distinct from the original 2D row/column cleaning problem):  
A particle moves on a 1D line with integer positions from 1 to n (n <= 100). It starts at position `start_pos` with initial direction `dir = 1` (positive direction). Each step: compute tentative next position `next_pos = current_pos + dir`. If `next_pos < 1` or `next_pos > n`, reverse `dir = -dir` and set `next_pos = current_pos + dir`. Find the minimum steps t >= 0 for the particle to reach `target_pos` (1 <= start_pos, target_pos <= n). Assume it always reaches. Input: first line t (1 <= t <= 10^4), then t lines each with n, start_pos, target_pos.

- **Generate Python3 code step by step to solve that problem**  
  Step 1: Import sys for fast input and read t.  
  ```python
  import sys
  input = sys.stdin.read
  data = input().split()
  index = 0
  t = int(data[index])
  index += 1
  ```  
  Step 2: Loop over t test cases, read n, start_pos, target_pos.  
  ```python
  for _ in range(t):
      n = int(data[index])
      start_pos = int(data[index + 1])
      target_pos = int(data[index + 2])
      index += 3
  ```  
  Step 3: Handle t=0 case immediately.  
  ```python
      if start_pos == target_pos:
          print(0)
          continue
  ```  
  Step 4: Simulate the movement with a loop, tracking position, direction, and time. Break when target reached. (Safe since n<=100, max steps O(n).)  
  ```python
      pos = start_pos
      dir = 1
      time = 0
      while True:
          time += 1
          next_pos = pos + dir
          if next_pos < 1 or next_pos > n:
              dir = -dir
              next_pos = pos + dir
          pos = next_pos
          if pos == target_pos:
              print(time)
              break
  ```  
  Step 5: Full code combines all (no max iter needed due to guarantee).  
  ```python
  import sys
  input = sys.stdin.read
  data = input().split()
  index = 0
  t = int(data[index])
  index += 1
  for _ in range(t):
      n = int(data[index])
      start_pos = int(data[index + 1])
      target_pos = int(data[index + 2])
      index += 3
      if start_pos == target_pos:
          print(0)
          continue
      pos = start_pos
      dir = 1
      time = 0
      while True:
          time += 1
          next_pos = pos + dir
          if next_pos < 1 or next_pos > n:
              dir = -dir
              next_pos = pos + dir
          pos = next_pos
          if pos == target_pos:
              print(time)
              break
  ```

- **Discuss the algorithm to solve this problem**  
  The algorithm uses direct simulation of the 1D bouncing movement, which is O(n) time per test case in the worst case (e.g., traversing back and forth across the line). Space is O(1). It correctly handles reflections by checking the tentative move and reversing direction only if out-of-bounds, matching the "reflect before movement" rule. Since the particle visits every position periodically (period ~2n), it guarantees termination. For small n<=100 and t<=10^4, total time is efficient (~10^6 operations). Alternatives like unfolding the line into an infinite path (mapping t to position via modulo 2n with reflections) exist but are more complex and unnecessary here.

- **Finally generate a planning to solve that problem**  
  1. Read all input at once using sys.stdin.read() for speed with multiple tests.  
  2. Parse t, then loop t times, reading n, start_pos, target_pos each iteration.  
  3. If start_pos == target_pos, output 0 and skip simulation.  
  4. Initialize pos = start_pos, dir = 1, time = 0.  
  5. Enter loop: increment time, compute tentative next_pos = pos + dir.  
  6. If next_pos out of [1,n], reverse dir and recompute next_pos = pos + dir.  
  7. Update pos = next_pos.  
  8. If pos == target_pos, output time and break.  
  9. Ensure no infinite loop via problem guarantee (add optional max_iter=10000 for safety).  
  10. Output for each test on new line.

### Algorithm to solve the original problem
- **Algorithm**: Decompose the 2D problem into two independent 1D bouncing simulations (one for rows, one for columns) because reflections and movements in row and column dimensions do not interfere. For rows: simulate starting at r_b, dr=1, bounds [1,n], target r_d to find first_hit_row (min t >=0 where row(t) = r_d). Similarly for columns: start c_b, dc=1, bounds [1,m], target c_d to find first_hit_col. The answer is min(first_hit_row, first_hit_col). Handle t=0 explicitly if r_b == r_d or c_b == c_d. Each 1D simulation is O(max(n,m)) time via loop until hit. Total per test O(n + m), across 10^4 tests: ~10^6 operations, efficient.  
- **Tutorials about the algorithm**:  
  - **How to approach this type of algorithm**: Identify independence in dimensions (common in billiard/reflection problems). Reduce 2D to 1D subproblems. Use simulation for small constraints instead of math formulas (e.g., unfolding grids for LCM periods). Validate with samples: trace positions manually for small n/m.  
  - **Important things to consider**: Reflection rule ("reflect before movement"): always compute tentative nr = r + dr, check bounds, reverse if needed, then use new nr. Initial dir always +1. t=0 cleaning before any move. Guarantee of reaching avoids infinite loops (but cap iterations at 2*max(n,m) for safety). Edge cases: n=1 or m=1 (stays put after flips), start == target (0), corners/ simultaneous hits (min handles). Independent sims ensure no missed early OR condition. Test with sample (e.g., first case min(row hit to 2, col hit to 8) =7).

### Plan
- **Step 1**: Read input efficiently: use `import sys; input = sys.stdin.read; data = input().split()` to parse all at once. Read t = int(data[0]), set index=1.  
- **Step 2**: For each of t test cases (loop t times): parse n, m, r_b, c_b, r_d, c_d from data[index:index+6], increment index by 6.  
- **Step 3**: Check initial position (t=0): if r_b == r_d or c_b == c_d, print 0 and continue to next test.  
- **Step 4**: Define a reusable 1D first-hit function (in plan, describe logic; implement as inline or def):  
  - Inputs: size (n or m), start (r_b or c_b), target (r_d or c_d), init_dir=1.  
  - Initialize pos = start, d = init_dir, time = 0.  
  - Since t=0 already checked (!=), start loop with time=1:  
    - Compute nr = pos + d.  
    - If nr < 1 or nr > size: d = -d; nr = pos + d.  
    - Set pos = nr.  
    - If pos == target: return time.  
    - time += 1.  
    - (Optional: if time > 2*size: error, but unnecessary.)  
- **Step 5**: Compute row_time = first_hit(n, r_b, r_d).  
- **Step 6**: Compute col_time = first_hit(m, c_b, c_d).  
- **Step 7**: Print min(row_time, col_time).  
- **Step 8**: Repeat for all tests, ensuring outputs on separate lines. No extra spaces/newlines.  
- **Step 9**: Verify with samples: e.g., last case same col ->0; first case sim rows from 6 to hit 2 (trace:6→7→8→9→10→9→8→7 (t=7? match 7)), cols from1 to8, min=7.  
- **Step 10**: Edge handling: n=1/m=1 (time=0 if match), start at boundary (e.g., r_b=1 dr=1 → next=2), diagonal corner bounces (indep so ok).

- **Step 4**: 1D first-hit logic (manual simulation, init_dir=1, start loop at time=1 since t=0 checked):
  - **Rows** (size=10, start=6, target=2, d=1):
    - time=1: nr=6+1=7 (valid), pos=7 !=2
    - time=2: nr=7+1=8, pos=8 !=2
    - time=3: nr=8+1=9, pos=9 !=2

    - time=6: nr=9+(-1)=8, pos=8 !=2

    - time=8: nr=7+(-1)=6, pos=6 !=2

    - time=10: nr=5+(-1)=4, pos=4 !=2

    - time=12: nr=3+(-1)=2, pos=2 ==2 → row_time=12
  - **Columns** (size=10, start=1, target=8, d=1):
    - time=1: nr=1+1=2 (valid), pos=2 !=8
    - time=2: nr=2+1=3, pos=3 !=8

### Plan Evaluation
**No Need to Modify Plan**.
```python
import sys

def first_hit(size, start, target):
    if start == target:
        return 0
    pos = start
    d = 1
    time = 0
    while True:
        time += 1
        new_pos = pos + d
        if new_pos < 1 or new_pos > size:
            d = -d
            new_pos = pos + d
        pos = new_pos
        if pos == target:
            return time

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    m = int(data[index + 1])
    r_b = int(data[index + 2])
    c_b = int(data[index + 3])
    r_d = int(data[index + 4])
    c_d = int(data[index + 5])
    index += 6
    
    row_time = first_hit(n, r_b, r_d)
    col_time = first_hit(m, c_b, c_d)
    print(min(row_time, col_time))
```
